=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Classic Knizia auction game, that also involves set collection, where your points are your money, and you use them wisely to generate more points.

Each round, you auction tiles of goods that are to be acquired and put onto boats, which are space-limited.  The set you auction is up to that player's discretion, and this is where alot of the strategy is, in the set offerings.  The highest bid takes the set, and all players continue until everyone has five total tiles.  The highest tile total(s) score points.  Then the goods are loaded onto the ships- the highest(s) in each ship also score points, then you can also score extra points for moving exceptionally high within a boat.  3 rounds and the game is over.

I've heard that this game is only good for 6 (which makes it valuable for that sake alone, not many good 6-player games), but definitely enjoyed my 4-player games of this, so I think it probably scales well, plus it's relatively short and easy to learn/play.

Copy signed by R. Knizia at GenCon '07.

RATING: 7
2 plays / 4 players

LIKE: Such an excellent design. I really enjoy the subtle constraints with the boat size, the 10 gold card, the goods track, and deciding how many cards come out. The once-around auction works very well here.

DISLIKE: Difficult for beginners to know the value of cards and when to bid to block someone. Dry theme-- I think it could have been a pet store theme to attract a more diverse audience. Points can be somewhat predicted after a few plays, but the random cards work to diffuse this.

OVERALL: Solid game, plays quickly, easy to explain. Lighter than Ra, but more accessible. 

Comparable to Stockpile.

RATING: 6
One of the classic bidding games, I have the updated illustrated version which is lovely. The rules are so simple, the entire game truly hinges on your ability to maneuver your opponents into poor bids or slink in to gain a set of cards for a low price - or even free if played right. I love that it extends from 2 to 6 players as well. However, it is a fairly sterile experience unless you really enjoy a pure bidding conflict.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A gem of a game that I'm just starting to get to know.  The most recent version of the game by Rio Grande has decent artwork and the box looks quite opulent.  I know some of the previous incarnations have been dreadful, but this one is fine.  The board could do with being 10% bigger though as the ppieces don't go on very well - or as a friend of mine said - pegs and a hole board would be a good way of scoring the markets.

The game itself could be viewed (if you were so inclined) as rather mathematical, yet I didn't find myself attempting to calculate every single nuance as I might have done in Modern Art.  I think that the maths is obscured by the fact that you're hardly ever sure what is left in the bag or how your position within the various scoring components is going to come out each round. Thus the overall feeling is of a rough calculation rather than a precise one...and this is greatly to the game's benefit.  So, it's a much simpler game than Modern Art, yet loses nothing for it.  It's also really elegant, plays in 40-50 mins and is a lot of fun. I didn't find it dry at all.  I think it will get a lot of play when there is 50-60 mins left in a session.

We always have loads of repartee and joking playing the game.  This is aided by calling the skins "hedgehogs," the spices "ganja" (you can imagine the crop of comments there) and the cloth "flying carpets."

RATING: 8
Auction + Set Collection = Fun!  (At least in this case.)

A rather fun game where cards are purchased with VPs via an auction mechanism.  There are 3 rounds with scoring at the end of each round based on the total value of the cards (the numbers, 0-5 and 1x 10) and leaders and/or higher placements in sets of the cards (the 5 colors).

The theme is pretty thin, at least in the version with cards, but don't let that deter you.  This is a fun auction game that provides many interesting choices – valuation of the cards, whether to bid or pass, how many cards to auction when you lead, which cards to collect, denying others certain cards, and so on.

Now I'm not a huge fan of auction games in general, but I gave Medici an 8 because if I'm going to play an auction game it might as well be a good one like this.

RATING: 7
A simple, streamlined, and intuitive auction game that holds up decades later. In this game, players are bidding on "lots" of goods with 2 end-of-round scoring mechanisms in mind. The first end-of round scoring simply rewards players with the most valuable cargo, irregardless of the goods. The second end-of-round scoring rewards players who have accumulated more goods, of a certain type, than their opponents. And because the second end-of-round scoring carries into future rounds, players quickly become invested in certain goods. Thus, the valuation of a lot is variable depending on the players and where they are in turn-order. Furthermore, the game has a spatial element (boats can only carry so many goods) and gives the auctioneer control over how large the lots area, which adds another interesting dimension to the game. 

Medici is often compared to Ra, another auction game by Knizia. And while Ra is often seen as more of a gamer's game because of the numerous ways to score... I actually prefer the simpler Medici because its less chaotic. For instance, both games have variable round lengths... but that is dictated by player decisions in Medici whereas its dictated by randomly drawing certain tiles in Ra. And as you can imagine, knowing the length of a round can have a profound effect on the valuation during the game.

Furthermore, Medici is known for some horrible editions. Somehow, this game has been printed by numerous publishers and they've largely have done a poor job with the production quality. And while the Latest edition by Grail Games is one of the best editions, it still falls short for being ideal; most notably, the money/vp track is difficult to read and the colorization of the goods/player-pieces isn't as distinctive as it should be. Who knows, maybe one day this game will be printed to a high standard. 

My only strong criticism of the game comes with lower player counts. With less players, cards are randomly removed from the game. And with some player counts (2/3-players) you may face a situation where 1/2 the cards aren't in play. Thing is, the known-distribution really adds richness to the decisions and having so many cards randomly removed adds a huge luck factor. So if you do play with less players, use one of the variants that keeps the distribution relatively-known.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Medici is one of the purest auction games form the auctions wizard: the doctor Reiner Knizia.

It has a very simple rule set, and the result of the game depends only on the (strainghtforward but not simple at all) bidding decisions of the players.
The once a round bidding forces players to evaluate lots immediately, but each lot has different value to each player...

It is a filler, but a very thinky one. And it works with 6.

Tentative rating is a very respectable 7.5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A game with Jekyll and Hyde exteriors for housing the same brilliant mind. Be careful which Medici you add to your own games gallery!

p: We first played this on a convention in the Amigo version, and would happily have played that game alone for the remainder of the day. A simple and elegant mechanic transformed into an utterly brilliant bidding game allowing for a wealth of interesting strategies, all viable. The element I enjoy most is letting people win a large amount of cards so you can force them out of the bidding of subsequent card sets by revealing one more than they have left room for.

One of the top 2 or 3 Knizia's and we were delighted when someone brought the US remake along to our weekly meet-up, so we could indulge once again, and to my surprise it became a very frustrating experience.

I seriously feel that this could well be the only game to deserve 2 entries, one for the original German Amigo one, and one for the Rio Grande version. How else to record that the likelihood of me playing this game again has an Atlantic 6 point rating gap between versions? Amigo 9, Rio Grande 3. It is the same game!
 
As I understood it, when Rio Grande licensed the game, they forgot to license the artwork. So they got a graphic designer to do that for them, who no doubt took one look at the theme and grabbed the pastels, feeling all DaVinci. And probably dreamt of matching the master when the boring coloured-bar pyramids disappeared in favour of superimposed artwork from the cards. Fantastic! Right up until the point you put dobbers on them and you realize that identifying areas are either cropped out or simply covered up. And suddenly it all becomes clear as mud. The first two pyramids alone might as well have had the same artwork, causing most of the confusion, but there seemed to be disorientation around the table whichever cards were revealed. Confusingly the cards have very distinctive coloured borders. Which correspond to absolute nothing on the board. Why?

Problem is, a clear overview of people's relative positions on the pyramids is vital to the game. If you can't take stock with a simple glance you add another layer of dilemmas that even the good Herr Knizia wouldn't welcome.

And like all warm relationships, once what was the main attraction becomes an irritant, everything gets dragged up, and suddenly the perfect match feels like one catastrophic mismatch. An anti-clockwise scoring track has to be dreamt up by a non-gamer. And I have seen players quite innocently skip the corner squares when moving their score-marker forwards (and backwards). Since only the odd numbers are visible, those beacon squares like 10,20,30,etc are ever so hard to zoom into. A few other points came up, and it was generally agreed that Rio Grande couldn't have made the presentation worse if they had tried it. Until someone pointed out that there was still the option to replace the playing pieces with round marbles.

Great graphic design usually is at its best when you are not aware of it, something that the German publishers quietly are very good at. One shot at the Rio Grande edition made me re-appreciate this often ignored skill.

Be sure to pick your right bedfellow if you want to have fun playing...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Falls short of Modern Art and Ra in the Knizia auction trilogy.  The auctions aren't nearly as compelling and luck plays a much larger role.  Yet, what kills it is how much policing needs to go on among the players for any semblance of an interesting ang tight game to emerge.  I don't consider myself nearly as sensitive to such issues as many on BGG, but Medici almost builds it into the game.  I guess there is something to be said for placing a "perfect" bid that will send your opponent into paroxysms of agonizing analyzation, but too often it degrades into someone having to take it upon themselves to bid up a particular lot to make a player pay a "fair" price.  The difference between Ra and Medici is that while both make the items auctioned of different worth to each player, Ra provides more constraints on what is auctioned and the bidding.

RATING: 7
Didn't get to play this game until recently and was surprised how fun it was being 22 years old. Bidding games are not my forte, but this one I like to play more.

It reminded me somewhat to Ra, another bidding game of Knizia, that I actually played a lot and I like Medici way better. The set collection is cleaner and the press-your-luck element isn't as prevalent as it is in Ra. Ra could get really swingy, this game has more of a gradual buildup.

What's also nice is that you can play a six player game in under an hour and still have a bit of a challenge. I would say this game works better with higher player counts, because of the distribution of resources.

I think this one deserves to be labeled as a classic by now. It's held its own.

RATING: 7
An auction game with all of the wonderful crabs-in-a-bucket meta-play.  There are 3 rounds.  Each round there's cumulative 5 area majority scoring as well as single biggest value scoring.  The biggest value scoring is great er than any area majority but runs counter to focusing on a single (or two) areas.  Also, there are lovely opportunities to encourage others to fall on their swords.  

Brilliant play and made it into my permanent collection after a single play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Juego puro de apuestas. Se apuesta por losetas, a una sola ronda, por lotes de una, dos o tres losetas de hasta cinco mercancías distintas, con el objeto de incorporar el lote en el barco de cada jugador. El barco tiene capacidad para cinco losetas. Una vez que se llena quedas fuera de las pujas.

Cuando todos los barcos estan llenos, se evalua primero el valor total de las mercancías (las losetas vienen con valores del 0 al 5) y se reparten 30,20,10 y 5 puntos según la posición.

Después en el tablero cada uno incrementa su nivel de cada mercancía (según el número de losetas) y también, en cada categoría se reparten puntos. En determinados niveles además se reciben puntos adicionales.

Es un juego sencillo de jugar y explicar.. aunque tiene pujas. Se le puede equiparar al Ra, aunque en éste se controla la duración del juego estupendamente.

Recomendable y accesible para no jugones.

RATING: N/A
Pendant la Renaissance, Florence la grande rayonnait dans le monde entier. Dans son port, les bateaux arrivaient du bout du monde, apportant avec eux quantité de trésors : étoffes, épices, métaux précieux, porcelaine... Dans la peau d'un marchand florentin, vous cherchez a devenir le plus riche. Au cours de vos voyages, vous devrez intelligemment dépenser vos deniers pour acheter aux encheres les bonnes cargaisons (la taille de vos cales étant limitée) et les revendre de retour dans votre cité. Au fil de trois saisons de commerce, vous chercherez a avoir le monopole sur certains produits.

RATING: 4.5
Medici finds itself - at least as far as I'm concerned - in an uncomfortable position among auction games. It does not offer the sheer fun and chaos of Kuhhandel, nor is it as strategic as Ra. Further, given it has no theme to speak of, it cannot compare to the wonderfully themed Traumfabrik (haven't yet played Modern Art, so can't compare it to that acclaimed title). 

I don't see what the repeat draw of Medici is compared to those three auction games. Dry themewise and calculational. Not bad, but there are simply better games out there these days.    

In short I consider Medici superceded by later Knizia designs. He - as the whole German boardgame scene - has come a long way since this release.

RATING: 8
A brilliant straight-forward (meaning easy to grasp the rules of) multiple variable auction game.

However, like many rules light games with great depth, if you don't see the depth, you risk giving the game away to those who do as you play at the most obvious level (letting people get lots for cheap, letting someone fill up their boat for free in the first round, letting someone corner a market in the first round and max out in the second).

I feel the game works best at six players due to the pigeonhole principle forcing competition and the absolute knowledge of what is available .

RATING: 7
Another relatively short and simple auction game; in this one, you bid your score for sets of items, and in each round, you stop bidding when you fill up your ship with five goods.  However, not only do the types of goods you buy matter (having the most of a type of good rakes in points), but the point values on the cards matter each round.  A few different strategies--or combinations of strategies--can be employed to collect points and try to win.

RATING: 8
(about 30 plays)... Excellent game! Feels like a classic. Highly recommended. Yes, go buy it! I enjoy it quite a bit. Seems quite different between numbers of players, since you remove cards with less than 6 people. A couple friends complained about the too-small components, the cubes that fit narrowly on the board's pyramid levels, and that the stylized little cards are hard to read across the gaming table. I think the common criticisms of the visuals are justified. Also, some of my gaming-newby friends, who learned the game at the same time as me, had trouble understanding how to play competitively, and liked the game less for that reason. I'm not lowering the score, however, since the rest of us loved it. I can't wait for the new version to come out (I pre-purchased). The new design (radial board, with tiles) looks promising. This game doesn't seem to mix well with tequila (madness), but I have played successfully at a beer pub.  Update: Speaking as a fan and advocate of Medici, the new board's radial design is easier to comprehend, a real achievement.  I might like the previous version's cards a little better; the tiles are OK though, they work.  The new commodity pictures are more recognizable, but not perfectly so.  And the previous scoring track is easier to manage than this one, but here you can stack the player tokens.  As scores separate, it isn't a big problem. I've also played on the French version and thought it quite beautiful but again, commodity board spaces are too small, and it suffers a little from no player mat ship cards, but the cards are superlative and clear. I have mixed feelings about the special power cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
There are so many competing forces at work in such a tight package with this game. Its easy to overlook the tremendous power at being the one who designates which lot(s) are up for auction. 

Having the most valuable boat is the most obvious and indeed a powerful way to get a lot of points. However, if a few lots go unclaimed, suddenly the prospect of getting stuck with empty holds in your boat becomes a real gamble...especially if you're trying to maximize your shipment's points. Nabbing some cheap cards to bump your commodities up the chart can be another viable way to score fairly big. 

Its tough to correctly value each card for yourself, let alone figure out the relative value to other players. Throw in the increasing stress of not filling your boat (which has a Ra-like push-your-luck element) and this game becomes VERY cut-throat. I LOVE it!

I just wish my older Rio Grande version wasn't so freaking UGLY. The scoring track is ludicrous. I dont' particularly like how the commodity towers are visually represented, but I can at least deal with that. I really would like to get one of the pretty new copies sometime. Its just hard to bring myself to spend/trade for a game I already have.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I have somewhat mixed feelings about this game. It's better when actually playing than when discussing or thinking about it. The board design is horrible in both editions though. Overall, I'd rather play Ra.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Short, yet interesting auction game by Knizia that is often overlooked. I like the decisions involved in choosing between high number domination vs. given suit domination, and the agonizingly low number of items that one can win during the game. I also like the auction mechanic wherein one can make a seemingly good lot rather wretched by adding another card to it, or set oneself up for a real bargain. The main knock I have on this the whole 'bidding with your victory points' thing again. I think it takes something away from the game and makes it more dryly mathematical than it needs to be. Also a little less variable than I would like in an auction game; but still worthy of several plays and very fun. Another Knizia auction game winner.

One play.  My rating is probably skewed because I do not take well to auction games.  But in this particular game the curve seemed even worse than usual.  I was completely out of it by the end of the first turn and spent the rest of the time watching as other players debated on whether or not to turn another card or whether or not to bid.  Sometimes a game like this can still be fun and exciting through theme or interaction but these game has little of either.  I also do not find the game aesthetically pleasing.  I wouldn't ask to play this again, however would be willing to play again if others wanted to.  I acknowledge that with repeated play and increased understanding of the dynamics of the game I might enjoy it more.

RATING: 9
[COLOR=#990000]**[Español** El juego mas matemático de Knizia junto con el MvsS y el Amun-Re.

Siempre he tenido muchas dudas sobre que juego era el mejor de subasta pura hechos por Knizia pero nunca lo había tenido claro. Con la aparición del Medici vs. Strozzi las dudas se me aclararon, insuperable ¿o no?

Al igual que mí comentario del Medici vs. Strozzi me complace el pensar que este juego es como una especie de Battle Line para gamers pudiendo jugar hasta 6 personas.

Las reglas francesas en la subasta es contínua, en las reglas de Rio Grande la subasta es a una sola vuelta. Otra diferencia es que cuando haces el lote de hasta 3 cartas no puedes sacar más de los huecos que te queden en el barco, con las de Rio Grande sí puedes; yo recomiendo las reglas de Rio Grande.

Si te gusta este juego también es conveniente que le eches un ojo a los siguientes: Schotten Totten, Battle Line, Lost Cities, Medici, Marco Polo Expedition y la serie de 10 days.[/COLOR]

RATING: 10
Components: Nice boat mats and commodity markers, but my English version has cards with difficult-to-read numbers, lack of color coordination between the cards and their corresponding score tracks on the board, and the most awful score track I've seen, with every other number skipped. One of the few productions that interfere with game play.

The 2006 Rio Grande edition is prettier, and I like drawing the tiles from the bag, but the score track is too small, the markers too oddly-shaped, and the pyramids confusing.

Experience: Game play is great, with three tense rounds of bidding and acquiring my goods. The two ways of scoring give each card a different value to each player, which keeps the bidding unpredictable. This gets better with each play. Works well with 3-6 players.

RATING: 7
Excellent auction game. It is short yet very thoughtful and strategic for a short game. You get one shot to name your bid, that's it. Too low, you don't get another shot and someone else walks away with the goods. Too high means you fill up your hand and you burn away your VP's. One of the few HEAVY fillers I know of. Highly recommended. Very much in common with poker, the more you play the game the more evident this fact becomes.

RATING: 10
In this game you must evaluate the likelihood of getting majorities in competing sets. And you must guess how others will evaluate those same likelihoods. I love both activities, so I adore this game. Bids are tense, rounds are varied, player information is visible, but upcoming information isn't. One downside is the learning curve, which may cause new players to incorrectly value lots and fail to reign in their opponents. But when all players are good at evaluating lots, the game is wonderfully tense.

At any rate, I found it somewhat unsettling that I was able to shoot from last to second on a single round simply by gambling on 5 random tiles, a tactic that also worked in the first round for another player.  Either everyone else was playing wrong or this is simply just what happens in the game.  Either way, with such huge scoring swings, I find it hard to recommend this one.

That being said, I'd play it again, so it's not like I didn't have a good time.  I just find it hard to say this game is good.

RATING: 7
Medici is an excellent game with 6 players. I recently played Medici twice in one evening. Both games were fun and tense.

Traded a copy.

In a recent 6 player game, we were having a blast in the 2nd and 3rd round. It was an extremely intense game.

The fact that you bet only once for the lot of cards is a real thrill. It makes  Medici a lot more interesting than having multiple bids and always over bidding the players like some other auction/bidding games.

In Medici, trying to judge the worth of the lot of cards is great. Is it worth more to you or your oppenents? How much the worth in both cases ? Giving a lot almost for free or paying too much can be a turning point in the game.

I played Medici once with 3 players. I found Medici equally interesting as in a 5 or 6 player game. I was surprise how a 3 player game could be as fun as a 5 or 6 player game. Very enjoyable game overall even though not all the card are been used. The fact that with fewer players, do you take the lot on the table or skip it and hope the card(s) that you want comes out? Hard choice sometimes.

Own two copies. The old one and new one. Traded newer version.

10 plays since recording games played.

RATING: 8
Medici is a Knizian auction game with a once a round bidding mechanism with 3 scoring rounds, similar to Ra. There is even a little push-your-luck aspect to the game as well, but not nearly as much as in Ra. In fact, I would almost call Medici the gamer's version of Ra since lots are tougher to evaluate than Ra and you're bidding with your victory points. That said, one doesn't really replace the other. Even discounting Medici probably being more of a gamer's game, Ra works best with 3 - 4 and Medici with 5 - 6. I've liked the look of all versions of Ra, but Medici's graphic design over various iterations hasn't been the best. This is so true that I decided to make my own board for my copy of the game. If you like auctions, tough decisions, and games that accommodate higher number of players, you owe it to yourself to give Medici a try.

Special note on the French Lui-Meme edition: My board was the worst warped game board I've ever received. Tried to contact them via e-mail for a replacement and it was returned to me as it didn't work. 

[url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/2917532/medici]Custom Board I Made[/url]

RATING: 4
[1 play] I think the greater interaction and uncertainty with more players made this less awful than Medici vs. Strozzi.  However, it was a great reminder of why I don't like the auction genre much.  This game is just pure auctioniness, in much the same way that Quo Vadis is pure negotiation.  I like auctions OK when they're in a context that gives them some meaning.  Like in Scepter of Zavandor--when there's an auction, I know what I want to buy, and why I want it, and how much I'd pay for it, and what I'm going to do with it when I get it, because all that relates to the other 90% of the game.  Scepter of Zavandor is like a beautiful piece of music, and Medici is like playing scales.  There's no point to it; it's just all about valuing stuff and manipulating the auctions.  I know there are people who find that delightful, but I am not among them.

Regardless of one's feelings about auctions, though, the art design of this game deserves to have some time and space given to denouncing it.  Visually, this game is really poorly done.  There are five commodities that are shown on cards and in five pyramid-shaped pictures on the board.  On the board, they aren't clearly labeled with their names; you have to pick them out by comparing the picture, which you can only see part of, because it's a pyramid-shaped piece of a rectangular picture.  The cards are color-coded, with each commodity having a different color background--but those colors aren't used on the board, so they don't help a bit to match things up.  The names are written on the cards, but in a scrawly decorative script that's not at all easy to read.  The number values on the cards are also in a hard-to-read font and superimposed on top of other stuff.  Given that you're going to spend the game looking around at other people's cards to see what they have and what's been played, and adding up the values of the cards on their ships, it is really annoying to have them not be easier to see/read.  I'd also approve of the score track more if it were numbered on every space instead of every other space.  All in all, the visual design of the game isn't unattractive, but it's definitely not very functional.

One convention of play that we did not use, but that I think would make the game better, is to score stuff as you put it on your ship.  Once you have a card on your ship, it's inevitable that at the end of the round, you'll move up on the pyramid for that commodity.  So you might as well go ahead and do it.  That way, during the round, you're not constantly thinking, "If I buy this lot, it will put me two up in wheat.  That would put me ahead of Alice, but she's also got a wheat on her ship, so we'd be tied.  And Bob has bought two wheat, which will put him one ahead of me.  Etc."  You can just look at the board and see the up-to-the-minute standings and do your calculations from there.  It removes a whole layer of thought and delay, not to mention squinting at cards trying to figure out what they are.

RATING: 6
A great pure auction game for when you want that type of experience. The three round limit is perfect for a game of this type as any longer would make the game too long relative to the depth of decisions you can make. At first the decisions seem plentiful. Should I put up another item for auction? Should I diversify or pursue just a couple items exclusively? Should I overbid/underbid/not bid and try to get the free stuff? However, it seems the best strategy is to hang back, not bid and see what you can get for free. It seems as viable a strategy as fighting other people for known lots. 

I like an auction mechanism in games, but I've determined that I don’t like games that are only about an auction as they get stale. Ostensibly, the auction keeps the experience fresh, but without an action system to tether it to, it simply doesn't hold my interest. Moved this one on after a handful of plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Yet another set collection bidding game by Knizia, and yet again different enough to be worth it. With very little in the way of bells and whistles this pure auction game is about as lean as they come. Players bid on lots of goods that they (hopefully) turn into profits. As the value of a certain good depends on how heavily you are already invested in that good and there are at least two different value dimensions what is a load of junk to one player may be a godsend for another. The auctions are only once-around so figuring out the values of the lots for the other players is quite important, too.

RATING: 7
Medici is one of the more freewheeling auction games I've played.  It's quite difficult on your first play to know how much one should be bidding, so you end up bidding quite disparate amounts of money/points in order to win the goods. In the end, there are quite a few other bidding games that I like (I'm not a huge fan of once-around bids anyway) but Medici is different at least and doesn't have any serious faults (except poor graphic design) so I'd play it if anyone asked.

RATING: 8
Medici is more straightforward and more analytical than Knizia's very best auction games (Ra, Modern Art, Taj Mahal, Traumfabrik).  But it's also probably Knizia's most versatile auction game, both in terms of the number of players and in terms of the breadth of its appeal to different groups.  I think I like the five-player game best, although Medici scales well for any number from three to six.

Avoid the early Rio Grande edition with its horrible graphic design, and seek out either the Amigo edition or the newer French edition (which I think will also be carried by Rio Grande).

RATING: 7.99999
The best thing about this little bidding game for me is the way each player hangs on the edge of his or her seat while every other player calculates how much to bid. Most multi-player games have significant lapses of non-active player required attention, this one has every player riveted on every other's bid. Not a lot else going for it though and certainly no relevance to the historic period it references, but always a good in-between game for up to six. Great taste, less filling. Good entry level or introductory game.

RATING: 8
I do enjoy games where you have to evaluate the future value of items, and Medici is no exception.  I also enjoy the way in which you have to adapt your strategy depending on the apparent mind-set of your opponents - new players and their apparently wild bids can throw a strategy designed to beat experienced players.

I traded this game away despite liking it because I prefer the two player implementation: Medici vs Strozzi.  For multiplayer auction/evaluation games, Modern Art clinches it for me when you have an hour or two, and For Sale when you have fifteen minutes.

RATING: 7.5
It plays six people in under an hour! What a deal.

An auction game where money is points. Each round you can obtain five tiles via auction. The active player draws 1-3 tiles from the bag and decides when to start the auction. It is a one-time around auction.

The main things to consider is the color of the tiles--collecting a lot of the same color and/or having the majority in a color will lead to bonus payouts. Also the value of the tile--the most "expensive" collection of tiles will receive bonuses in descending order.

RATING: 6
A fairly early increment of the Knizia auction game, and if you follow the path Modern Art-Medici-Ra-Taj Mahal you soon see that the trend is one of increasing complexity. This is more critically dependent than most games on the number of players. With smaller numbers the game becomes increasingly luck based as more cards are randomly removed from the deck, with 6 you know exactly what's left. With precisely 5 playing this is more fun than most auction games, but Modern Art remains my favourite.

RATING: 8
Interesting! Ra without the training wheels.  As a heavy but short auction game for up to six players, this does a great job of filling a niche that is important to me. Being able to make money from either the majorities or the values (or both) means that there are several valid ways to go about the game,  some players may be doing better than they initially appear to be, and that, as the game develops, different players may value tiles differently.  I appreciate all these traits in an auction and everything that Medici gives me to think about. I like it better than Modern Art because the values are less calculable--though paying the bank instead of other players makes it a little less.. swingy. (8 plays)

RATING: 7
Played my 2nd and 3rd game of this on 7/25/07 which was a good experience. It's a quick teach (~5min) and less than an hour to play for 5 people, around 30 min for 3. I felt very engaged in the game and found myself saying "it's over now?" at the end wanting a bit more. My first experience (1st printing) was not good. The board was busy and hard to read. The cards didn't match the board. Fast forward several years and the cards are replaced with tiles. Personally the card idea was better being easier to shuffle and extract some cards. Making the cards match would have been a better alternative. The square boxes were replaced with spices and roll around too easy enough on the board. One dropped and scattered a few without touching anything. If you could lay the pieces flat, that would fix the issue. The board layout much better in the 2nd printing. The game itself is very enjoyable. There is a good mix of luck and strategy. Not one set strategy that works either, which is a plus. I'd recommend giving this a game play, especially if you know someone who has it. It's worth consideration playing on a regular basis if you like bidding games. All bets are off, if it's the first printing. I'd like to see a 3rd with new board, old squares, and old cards matching more closely.

RATING: 7.5
Apr09: My first play seemed pretty smooth.  It went fast with new players and had a lot of good parts.

I just wasn't expecting the huge role of luck in this game.  When one player got to fill their boat for free, they managed to draw the 10 and the two fives in the color they needed.  That just blew them out of the water in the final scoring.  Left a rather sour taste in our mouth.

I really need to try it with family though since that is what I bought it for.

There is great tension between trying to get similar colored goods and big value goods.  You can't do both and the balance act is tough and enjoyable.

Update:  Tried it with 6.  Very awesome.  Luck was pretty minimal actually.  Just enough of it to add spice to life.  I realized that the bids also involve jockeying to get the "free" flips.

RATING: 6
Generally, I'll avoid any game with a hint of pushing your luck, but Medici fills a hole in my collection for a short game that accommodates 2-6 players, with enough mental stimulation to keep me awake, but not too much to scare away muggles.

I'll only recommend this game towards the end of the night after the main event, if we're short on time, or if everyone in a group of 5-6 insists on playing together. Games with more players are generally more chaotic and less strategic, and therefore less interesting to me, but at 6 players Medici uses the full deck of goods-cards which allows for more concrete evaluations and card counting. Strozzi may be a better "gamers" version of Medici, but it's also more complex, and less accessible for casuals due to the complexity.

:thumbsdown: tactical at the expense strategy(liberal/conservative dealing and bidding and prioritizing what goods to bid on relative to your rightmost opponents.)
:thumbsdown: lack of non-transitive/counter strategies

:thumbsup: short
:thumbsup: accessible
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Another stellar, brilliant and elegant auction game from the "doc" that is an absolute hoot with the right group and right amount of 'friendly tabletalk'. 5 or 6 best I'm sure but I could see ways to "make" it playable with less. Forget about theme, there isn't one and it isn't needed anyway. 

Really a great middle-weight pure auction game with some set collection and some "push your luck" element as well. Some other similarities to "RA" besides those in previous sentence: Slightly subtle strategy, fast pace, once around bidding and can be hard to judge values making for very tough choices but it plays fast and keeps everyone involved. Scoring is as usual very nicely done with a few ways to get points and lots of competition to get them. A dose of luck as in most of his games but also manageable like most as well. Having the option of flipping 1 - 3 cards adds a nice strategic and push your luck element. This is a simple game to learn and play but a challenge to play well. 

I see the rating rising with more plays... 

Strange that such a simple game can't be made with good components, I don't recommend the new french version either but the German version is good. One of the best games I know for 6 players.

RATING: 7
6 player auction game that works fairly well, that alone excuses any faults that the game may have, although they do somewhat affect my enjoyment of the game

faults:
1) it is possible to win by getting lucky on what cards come out after everyone else has filled their boats, just doing this once during the game and making sensible decisions the rest of the time can be the difference between winning and loosing.

2) later in a round when only 2-3 players are left needing cards, the competition for one type might be limited, potentially giving it to 1 player for close to nothing (the other plays that could make that player pay properly for it, my be sacrificing their chances of winning if they do so)

RATING: 6.5
Finally got to play this. I won by 3-5 points at the end of my very first game. Very close game. Pure bidding and a bit of set collecting are necessary. Evaluation is driven by other player's needs. You spend your victory points in the bidding rounds to get goods.

The redesigned board is nice, much easier on the eyes, but it could still use some tweaking to make the goods columns stand out more from each other (note: look at the symbols of the items, not the colors).

How did I like it though?...hmmm...not sure how to rate it yet; it played smoothly, went fairly quick, not bad at all, but it didn't grab me on whole or light me up for another go right off. I'll start it off with a 6.5, but that's provisional. 

I'll need to play it a bit more to rest on a rating. It's good, it's OK fun, it's got some good player vs. player bidding...it IS a pure auction game at heart though and you WILL have to be able to evaluate your retun on investment vs. needs of the other players; sometimes you will need to stop another player, because no one else has; at those times it's kind of like in multiplayer Attika vs. two player Attika...sometimes no one wants to be the one to have to stop the other guy from connecting.

RATING: 8
Medici is pure simplicity and excellence in design. There is a deck of 5 commodity types with values of 0,1,2,3,4,5,5. Each turn a player turns over 1-3 cards and they are distributed as a set in a once-around auction. This continues until all players' ship holds are filled or the cards run out. Players can hold exactly 5 cards in a round, so if the set is larger than they can take, they cannot bid that turn. Money is awarded in decending order from the highest valued hold and also commodity types collected are tracked throughout the game, with the most and second most in each commodity getting points each round. That's it. There is no additional end game scoring.

As with all successful pure auction games, this is about manipulating the auction set and influencing the other players to take substandard lots or to frame players out of an auction entirely. Since every lot differs in size and composition, value judgements are required every turn and that leads to some great and fun table talk. These are my favorite kind of medium-weight games - high interaction coupled with crafty decisions.

The production on the KS is very good. I have just a few criticisms, for those interested (this has been an ongoing saga with Medici since first publication):
- Peach and yellow player colors are WAY too close of a match. I mean, Peach??
- The numbers on the score track are hard to see. They are not differentiated enough from the background color.
- The tokens in the middle - which may have been stretch goals - seem to serve hardly any useful function. I know what they do, but what they do is not needed.

RATING: 10
The classic once-around auction mechanic. The combination of bidding for values as well as commodity types creates lots that have ever-changing values. You have to weigh what a given lot is worth to you, and what it is worth to your opponents. If something is worth 10 to you but 20 to your opponent, what do you bid? Bid 10 and he'll quickly snatch it for 11. Bid 20 and watch him stick you with an overpriced lot on your ship. And valueing lots is hardly precise; you have to use gut feel to work towards those 10 and 20 numbers. Scales well from 3 to 6, but 6 creates a "perfect information" dynamic because all 36 cards are left in the commodity deck. This game can be an incredible multi-participant battle of wits. One of my all-time favorites! This would be a '10' except that valuation of lots is so difficult that final scores are often not very close.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Each player has a ship that can store 5 cards.  The cards are of 5 different commodities types. The cards also have a value from 0 to 5.  There is one special "Gold" card; it is not a commodity, but has a value of 10.

In turn, each player reveals 1 to 3 cards off the stack (note: if you already have 3 cards in your ship, you can only draw 2, since that is your ship's maximum capacity). Bidding begins with the player clockwise of the dealer and ends with the dealer.  You cannot bid if your ship doesn't have room for the cards and you can pass.  If everyone passes, the cards are discarded...but this can only happen a few times as the deck size is limited.

At the end of the round (when everyone has filled their ships with 5 cards), for each card of a commidity you have, you move your color up that specific commodity pyramid.  You also check to see who has the largest ship by adding up the numbers.  Points are awarded to the highest ranking winners of each of the five commodity pyramids and the largest ship (ties being split).

A very elegant bidding mechanic with short and long term goals.  Do you try for the largest ship this round, or build one of your stronger commodity rankings (as there is a bonus to getting to the pyramid top)?  Do you pass on bidding for now, waiting for others to fill up their holds, removing them from being able to bid on later draws? But how many cards have been discarded at this point?  Would I be pressing my blind luck to discard a few more and risk running out of cards to put in my hold?  Those are questions running through my head.

The early part of the game is easy to max/min.  Towards the end of the game, you'll be examining player's commoditiy pyramid rankings, guessing what they'll be willing to pay for, and hoping to up the bid to make them pay for it, even if you don't want it.

Rating would be higher if everyone has experienced the game and are of equal skill, otherwise this game has huge runaway leader problems.

RATING: 7
One of Reiner's better auction games.  You have a virtual ship with five spaces and collect trade goods with values that range from 0 to 5.  You obtain the goods through an auction of 1 to 3 goods which you add to your ship.  If you have the highest value goods on a ship, you get a bonus, second place gets a lower bonus, etc.  Each good is scored as well with the player shipping the most goods of that type getting 10 points and second place getting 5.  If you advance far enough you get more bonuses.

I like the auctions in this game as it is once around and the "auctioneer" rotates each round.    
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Part of the Knizia auction trilogy.
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/wiki/page/Knizia_auction_trilogy

Seems to be a game worth evaluating on its own merits rather than in comparison to Medici vs. Strozi. 

On its own merits, the games seems to employ certain subtleties that make it shine in the same way that Ra, Modern Art, and Hollywood Blockbuster shine in their own right. 

Plays quickly and scales well with increasing number of players.

Exists in different versions. See the following article which discusses pros and cons: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/337237/help-distinguishing-between-the-2-older-card-style

There seems to be a lot of complaints regarding design. Many have suggested using kilgore's design (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/filepage/36337/medici-homebrew-remake) which would cost $12 for the canvas board and $10 for the cards. Tokens would be extra.

See this for a beautiful redesign: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/442939/medici

RATING: 7
09/29/99 Received. I have the 1999 RGG version. I added labels to the base of the scoring pyramids to better tell the commodities apart. Otherwise there's much confusion between spice, cloth and dye, due to the artwork using the same bright colors for each. 
As of 06/28/17 I've won 29 of 57 games played; 50.8%. A pretty good rate for a game that plays up to six. 

09/11/07 Played using the 2006 edition. It was nice to try the new version of Medici with the tiles, but I still prefer my 1999 version with the cards. Even though I've never been a fan of the hard to read fonts used on the cards in my set, overall I think it trumps the 2006 edition. I can't really think of anything I preferred about the 2006 version. Things I didn't like were: 1) tiles and a draw bag instead of easier to handle/shuffle cards, 2) the tiles are now "non-language specific" and therefore only have a commodity icon and number on them, 3) the wooden "spice bag" tokens are too large for the score track and commodity pyramids, 4) the score track only has numbers every five spaces, as opposed to every two, 5) because of the draw bag its hard to tell how many tiles are left, 5) the background color of the tiles, especially the green & blue, are so muted they tend to meld together at a distance, 6) and I've never seen a dock in a star pattern - quite bizarre. Still, even this version looks better than the 2005 French edition. That thing is butt ugly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Yes, it's another Knizia auction game. The go-around-come-around style of the auctions is one step up from blind bidding, and the decisions regarding how many cards to lay down versus how many you can still fit in your ship's hold are tough. Also current hold values weighed against the average across the four rounds makes for some agonising. And yet, it's oddly ascetic and uninvolving. Unlike the tension of Ra or the anticipation generated by Amun-Re, this is rather a sterile experience, but not a bad one. I like it. I'm far from loving it, though. Mathematical balance can be beautiful. It can also be sterile.

Another game with a truly terrible board, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Game in a Nutshell: Buy tiles in an Auction to fill the limited space on your ship. Tiles score you points in a couple different ways.

Played as: 3 player

Pros: Not an overly complicated game but one that is not easily mastered. Most likely a finely balanced game for players dedicated to plowing through the learning curve.

Cons: The biggest problem with the game is how hard it can be for players to wrap their heads around the abstract values of the tiles they're bidding on. There's nothing to simplify it in players minds like in RA (is it worth one of the few optional bids I have?) or Vegas Showdown (it's going at a bargain of its established starting bid) leaving new players to fuddle through each auction. I'm sure over time players could mathematically get a feel for it, but why? There's plenty of other fine auction games out there to fill the void.

Other: I played the game once and knowingly with the least optimum number of players. Still, the major problems that I recognized didn't feel like they would change no matter how many players are present. I originally had interest in this game after playing RA and was convinced I must like auction games after some initial doubt. Well I was wrong, this is an example of how not to do an auction games. Either give players a reference of value or limit their options to simplify the decision making.

Best Use: For pure, unrestrained auction fans.

RATING: 7.75
Played once with four people.  A Knizia-special wherein players are bidding for items (5 types) that have different values (6 values in each of the types).  Players can get up to 5 items each round, and at the end of each round get points for having the highest total value of items as well as for having the most items of each type.  There are two big twists: 1) the currency used for bidding is victory points, and 2) players only get to place one bid on each lot (thus bidding order plays a large factor in the game).  There are very few extra cards, so someone is guaranteed to end up with low value cards.  A good mix of luck with strategy/tactics that requires thought yet isn't too long; I definitely want to play this more.

RATING: 3
After quite a few plays of Medici it slowly becomes apperant that almost nothing that the players attempt to do has much of a true effect on the game. True fact of the matter is that Medici's winner is almost entirely and completely dictated by luck of the draw. Even the greatest efforts that one makes cause you to only be successful as the cards that you pull when you have the power to say the final vote.

Honestly I originally enjoyed this game and thought it was good but over time you realize that your ability to affect and influence the end result is perpetually undermined by the luck of what you draw and what others draw on their turns. So really, what's the point in playing a game that you can't even win by your own effort? Might as well just sit around drawing lots; oh wait, that is what you are doing...

RATING: 6
A very solid auction game. I can see why some people consider this one of Knizia's best. The rules overhead is very simple, and while I think that the board is a bit superfluous, the decision space is vast. There's majority scoring, which I normally don't like, though it works well here. You also can't overlook how much money you'll get from having the most valuable ship. I really like that the points spread is dependent on the players, so this game caters to newbies and experienced players alike. There is no "right" way to play this, and the game can evolve with the players. This is something most auction games fail to do, but Medici does it with flying colors. This is the ultimate gateway game to auctions, though I still prefer Ra.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
An old Knizia game where one is trying to fill their boat with valuable commodities. Each turn, 1 to 3 cards are flipped up which people bid to load on their boat. The cards represent six different commodities and range in value from 1 to 5. There is also a commodity neutral card with a vaule of 10. Players can load a maximum of 5 cards onto their boats. Points awarded for largest value boats and the same type of commodities. There are some tactics, such as being the only one being able to hold 3 cards, flipping 3 cards, and only paying $1 for it. I know a lot of people like this Knizia classic (along with Modern Art), but I prefer Ra and Taj Mahal. Perhaps I'm trying to compare this game to those ones, but I prefer his games where there are multiple paths to victory. A bit dry to me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
[size=9][b][c]OVERALL REVIEW:     :star::star::star::star::star:[/c][/b][/size]
[size=8]A great auction game with some take-that forcing of the hand. The double scoring method for the cargo values and separately, the cargo goods, plus the bonuses at the top of each warehouse track, are all brilliantly simple game design. Every player attempts to strategize their bids with the potential dominant scoring opportunities. All the while, every auction is random, and clever players can force you into purchasing something beyond its value or urge you to press your luck with the next auction.
[color=green]My favorite auction game.[/color][/size]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've never really warmed to this one. It's a series of once around auctions after the active player turns over 1, 2 or 3 cards (valued 0 to 5 in 5 suits) and puts them up for auction. The game has 3 rounds, and nearly all the cards come out each round. The aim is to get the highest valued collection, plus to get first or second in the number of cards collected in each suit. Each player can only collect 5 cards in a round.

The problem is that if a great set of cards come out that suits one player but no one else, it's easy for everyone to decide not to risk falling on their own sword and allow that player to get it cheaply. If only one or two players are implementing the 'upping' skill, they risk damage to themselves and others gain at no risk. Especially as its a one shot bid. Therefore the winner can be the result of who is luckiest in getting the cheapest auctions because no one else is interested in that colour or whatever.

The plus is that there's lots of opportunity for ribbing, bagging and egging on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
-
Update 09 Aug 2007: reduced my rating from 7 to 6. Luck of the draw is so dominant that one can come back from way behind and win the game on the very last draw.

------------------------------------------------

I played this game with high expectations and was somewhat disappointed. A hallmark of an overtly luck based game is when you hear players saying things like, "Only if one of those two cards was a red", or "I would have scored 20 points more if instead of a '4' card, a '5' card had turned up." This is a frustrating game that makes you feel you have little control over your set of cards and thus your score. Of course planning and strategizing do matter but the right cards coming up at the right time go a long way towards helping you do well. 

The production of the board and the cards have some monor problems too. Some of the numbers on the cards are hard to read from accross the table without extra effort. But overall the game has good redeeming values, including: creating a sense of suspence and keeping you on your toes most of the time; very good player interaction; the satisfaction one gets from winning a 'bargin' auction, and pretty good replayability value. I enjoy playing the game periodically.  

-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
3 4 [ 5 6 ] joueurs
60 - 90 minutes

10 parties - Graphiquement, ce jeu (l'édition française) est peut-être le plus beau de ma ludothèque (avec mon jeu de Go). Le style moyenâgeux des cartes, apporte un cachet de "Classique" à ce jeu, comme s'il avait traversé les siècles pour venir jusqu'à nous. Les cartes sont si belles qu'elles me donnent envie de les réutiliser pour d'autres jeux chaque fois que cela est possible. J'aimerais posséder un jeu de 52 cartes du même genre. Félicitations M. Ehrhard !

Concernant le jeu lui-même, je suis toujours surpris quand je lis que certains le trouvent fun et rapide. Je ne sais pas y jouer sans me lancer à chaque enchère dans un calcul d'espérance de gain pour moi... et pour mes adversaires. Le jeu devient ainsi particulièrement long, calculatoire, aride, et même épuisant ! Je précise que je préfère les enchères à un tour, et vu le temps que j'y passe, ça vaut mieux.

Mon problème est que si ces calculs m'évitent de faire de grosses erreurs (et placent mes adversaires face à des alternatives indécidables), ils ne me permettent pas pour autant de gagner à coup sûr. Je comprends bien que je calcule une "espérance" de gain et que celle-ci n'est réalisée qu'en moyenne, toutefois tout ces bons calculs sont balayés dès que certaines situations incontrôlables surgissent telles qu'un dernier joueur qui acquièrent pour rien un lot de 3 cartes qui complète idéalement son chargement (s'il tirait 3 cartes sans intérêt, le résultat serait tout différent).

Un jeu à la fois très calculatoire et gouverné par le hasard ne me paraît pas une bonne combinaison, et ce jeu me laisse toujours perplexe même si j'aime bien y jouer de temps en temps, mais surtout pour avoir une bonne raison de manipuler ces cartes magnifiques. Le plus souvent, je préfère jouer à Modern Art, un jeu avec un vrai thème, vraiment fun, qui se contrôle mieux, et qui n'est pas plombé par des calculs savants.

RATING: 7
Medici suffers in my estimation in comparison to Ra. Like Ra, it has player-generated lots up for auction, the valuation of which varies widely between players. But what makes Ra brilliant is the discrete bidding tokens, which reduce the amount of calculation needed while making the decisions more interesting. Medici also has something of the push-your-luck element of Ra, for the person who stays in a round longest. But the risk (running out of goods because some have been jettisoned) seems to affect play much less than it does in Ra (all the Ra tiles being drawn before you get to bid). 

I've recently played several more games of Medici on my iPhone. I was enjoying it at first, though it does make it obvious how few decisions there actually are in a game. But I've now discovered that the AI opponents systematically over-value lots. I've won several times in a row by a wide margin, by simply refraining from bidding on almost everything. Filling your boat up for free is very powerful, combined with occasionally paying more for a lot that's just what you need. I guess the balance that comes in with real opponents is that if everyone tries to do this, lots of goods get jettisoned, which means you have to start bidding higher again to avoid getting locked out completely. 

But hey, it's still pretty good :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a very enjoyable game to play.  There are three rounds.  Each player has a ship card with five open holds for cargo.  Players take turns to randomly draw out 1 to 3 cargo cards from a bag  The cargo is then put up for bid.  Each cargo card has a value of 1 to 5 and one gold card worth 10.  You want to fill all the holds on your ship with cargo.  The round ends when all the cargo cards have either been purchased or discarded.  Scoring a round:  The ship with the highest value receives 30 points and depending on the number of players others may score.  After scoring the ships, the cargo is scored.  This is done by each player calling out the number of each product they have.  Your counter is moved up the ladder by one for each card.  The player at the top of the ladder scores 10, the second highest player scores 5.  Ties are split rounded down.

RATING: 8
Brilliantly simple game of auctioning. Balance ship capacity, goods majority bonuses, and cargo scores bonuses to earn the most money, all while trying to deny your opponents what they need. Choose wisely as you can only bid once per lot and ship space is at a premium. Overpay and you will lose, wait too long to make your move and you will lose, waste space on the wrong lot and you will lose. Plays very differently for 3 vs. 6. A great game from Knizia.

RATING: 10
ELEGANT. One of the best Euro games ever. The graceful auction element coupled with the limited resources cultivate a very competitive atmosphere. There are times to try to get the most valuable boat, but was it a smart move if you paid through the nose for it? The goals are more tangible (less abstract) than most German games and mesh well with the flavor. You want to dominate whatever commodities you can and strive for valuable boat values, and get them at a cheaper price than your opponents. Best when maxed out at 6, but plays great with as few as 4. Knizia's masterpiece! (see my article for this game for more details)

RATING: 7
Medici is one of those rare auction games that doesn't need a few dozen plays to get the hang of, valuation of lots is quite straightforward and it's as much about opportunity denial than it is about bagging the perfect lot.

As such, the beauty of the game lies in balancing going out early with something good for you or staying in long enough to make sure your opponents have to work hard for theirs.

... so yes, it's very very good, though I haven't really worked out the sweet spot in term of player count yet.

RATING: 7
Medici -- German edition preferred -- I'm a big fan of Rio Grande games, and the artwork in their first edition is quite nice, but because each trade account depicts its "ware" in multi-hued art, it can be difficult to differentiate the wares. The AMIGO edition uses art as a background, while each ware has a different color trade account, thereby making it easier to differentiate.

[i]Update 25 March 2006:[/i] Played a four-player game using the French edition. It has a beautiful board, and it depicts the wares on the scoring tokens -- smart, that. But the scoring track is half as long as the original, and since score is used for bidding, a longer scoring track is more desirable.

[i]Update 27 May 2007:[/i] The 2007 Rio Grande edition is quite beautiful.  Yes, the tiles make it more difficult to remove some tiles from play without seeing their faces, but their sturdiness makes up for that shortcoming.  The player tokens represent sacks (à la Goa) and while I prefer the custom ware tokens in the French edition, the sacks are still theme-appropriate.  However, the size of the tokens reduces their utility -- If they were half as thick and had a slightly smaller footprint, this would not be an issue.  I think the board layout is attractive, but one player complained that current standings were not as visually intuitive in this edition's concentric rings as in the original edition's horizontal alignment -- I have to agree.  The ware symbols on the board are too easily covered by tokens, making it difficult to distinguish which track represents which ware.  This would not be an issue if the ware colors were distinct from one another.  However, that is not the case.  It is far too difficult to distinguish the colors of the wares both on the ware tiles and on the board.  This edition comes so close to what it should be, but the color distinction issue is a deal-breaker.  The original AMIGO edition is still my favorite.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Possible RTW option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Played one 3P and one 5P game. Medici is a streamlined series of auctions, and the easiest to learn among Knizia's auction trilogy. In this game of majorities, players purchase 5 goods for their ship every round. At the end of every round, players score if they have the highest total ship values, as well as if they have the most of a certain good. 

As the optimum prices to bid on goods are more sharply defined as the round goes on, it creates a situation ripe for AP to occur. Thankfully, this burdensome analysis is somewhat reduced by the push-your-luck factor when revealing goods, which also adds some strategic considerations on when to flip over more cards. 

There is no denying that Medici is a fine pure auction game, but it involves more number-crunching than I would like. There's a reason why I enjoy Ra, as it hands you 3 discrete values to bid with during a round, while Medici requires you to fine-tune those values to do well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
2015: 8. Comments based on several games against the AI in the iOS version.  I investigated Knizia's auction trilogy (at least Ra and Medici) via iOS rather than trying to hunt down old copies of the game. I quite like both, despite the fact that both apps are in need of an update.

With Medici, you need more careful and canny bidding, as you can easily put yourself in the hole if you're not careful.  Sometimes you need to know when to call another player's bluff and force them to buy something they didn't really want!  I really like the dual objectives of trying to get the high boat, which is nearly mechanically disconnected from trying to maintain your first/second position in the goods, or pushing hard in one or two categories.

Of the two, I *think* I prefer Medici, but I need to play both games against humans to know for sure.  Looking forward to what the 2016 new edition looks like!

2016: UPDATE: 8. With the right group of people, this is a very strong game. Easy to explain, plays fast, beautiful to look at and is full of great moments of staring down your friends and trying to name their price juuuuust right.  Excellent game!

2017: UPDATE: 8.5. Have played now with a couple different groups of non-gamers/gamers, many who were unfamiliar with Knizia's designs. With one exception, everybody has really loved Medici and most said "let's play again!" immediately after!  Great addition to my collection, glad I got it!

RATING: 8
Probably the most thought-provoking auction game that I've played so far. Players only get one bid per lot, so there is a lot of tension. Bid too high and you limit your ability to outbid your opponents later on. Bid too low and you might miss out on the most valuable "cargo load" or bonus points for the most of a particular commodity. One wrong move in any of the three rounds and you can spoil your chances for victory. Unforgiving, but a fast game that is lots of fun...especially for an auction game.

RATING: 5
This one gets an extra point for playing six, easily, inside of an hour. This is yet another Knizia game where the system is set up in such a way that it's almost impossible to discern a decent strategy, and extremes of luck can really make or break someone's score. A good game, like all of his stuff, but not one that I like well enough to play often.

The best strategy is often just to do nothing. Save your money and let Lady Luck fill your boat for you. You'll beat the ones who spend every penny on the perfect boat every time.

For this reason, the two-player variant, Medici vs. Strozzi, is by far the superior game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Raised my rating from 8.0 to 8.5 - not my favorite game, but it is growing on me. The increasing need to get specific card types coupled with the need to claim high number cards to maximize bonus amounts makes the game feel more deliciously tight and tense, especially so with 6-player games.

Positives:  Plays extremely well with 6 players, a number that only a few games will even play and offers a very full gaming experience in a very short playing time without much complexity.  Ranks right up there with Web of Power in my book.

Caveat:  Try to get a new addition of this game if possible, since some of the older editions' game board graphics are fairly difficult to read and can lead to some player aggravation and errors in placement of markers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Set collection auction game set in Renaissance Italy. The game follows a number of auction where the active player reveals between one to three tiles, each depicting a type of good and a numeric value. Players then bid victory points to acquire the tiles (they begin the game with thirty). Once all the tiles have been auctioned scoring takes place, points awarded for having the highest numeric totals from the tiles and how far the goods have progressed the player along the corresponding goods' tracks. This is a simple game with the clever mechanism of having to sacrifice points in order to acquire them, and success depends on how efficiently this can be achieved. The theme however is completely tagged on giving the impression that the gameplay could have been applied to virtually any subject designer Reiner Knizia decided to choose (Renaissance period Italy always seems to do well in this regard).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
tl;dr: solid classic style auction game

Yes, believe it or not, I had never played this game before!
I know it's hard, but try to remember that time when there was a designer called [person=2][/person], and he was putting out classic after classic. Every new game with his name on it that was announced instantly went on any Euro gamer's radar. Man, that time is long gone, and sometimes I long for it. Just goes to show: I'm getting old.
So Medici was first put out at the height of his career. And it shows. This has everything a classic Reiner has: simple rules, well oiled play, nice depth of choices to make. Today he does the same except he develops his games to death so all fun is gone. I did find this game a bit long with 6, but the competition is very tight, and the auctions pretty tense. It made me remember why we used to love Reiner's designs.

Initial rating: 7/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 6.5

So; moderately luck heavy, but pretty fast to play with some difficult decisions and a few very straight forward decisions means that this is a decent, but far from spectacular game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The open-ended valuation of each auction reminds me of Modern Art. This is both a feature and a bug. It's not as dramatic as in Modern Art, but players in Medici can still bid themselves into oblivion unless they understand how to properly evaluate each batch. Do not expect to play this game well on your first go; in fact, assume you'll play poorly, especially in the first round. By the end of the game, you'll realize where you went wrong, and hopefully understand why you failed to bid high (or low) enough throughout the game.

Otherwise, I find this an intriguing game. The once-around auctions will be a love or hate type of feature. I love them, as they add tension and strategy based on player position. The freedom to bid as much as you like adds fragility, which will make repeat plays all the more enjoyable as players gain skill. I hope it lives up to my expectation after one play, and I look forward to playing again. This may be my favorite of the major Knizia auction games, as a perfect blend somewhere between Modern Art and Ra.*

As a final note, the graphic design and user interface are absolutely horrendous. Did the designer actually play the game?! Someone must have a better PnP option.


* I enjoy Modern Art and Ra, just not as much as Medici. I did not care for Dream Factory, and I have yet to play High Society.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
This game offers a lot of juicy choices: how many cards to add to the auction lot, whether to concentrate on ship value or goods control, whether to risk a bid on a lot you don&#039;t want or to risk letting the opponent to your left get it cheap, whether to hold off on bidding in hopes of getting a real steal at the end. The only reason I don&#039;t rate this an 8 is because the theme is a little dry; the board a little hard to read; and because as the once around bidding makes table position crucial,  if bad cards keep coming up when you have position advantage, you might not get the bargains other players get. The last is not really a flaw, but  a mechanic that can lead to frustration. Also, there is a small element of card counting, but you just need to make sure you look around the table to see what cards have been bought already.

RATING: 8
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/46/medici]Medici[/url] is an auction game where the players represent merchants vying for the best goods in the market to fill his ship.

The auction happen for lot of 1, 2 or 3 goods. The goods came in five different types: grains, dyes, fur, spices and cloth) and have number from 0 to 5.

The numbers in the goods serve to see, after the day (full round), which merchant has the most value ship. This is worth money (that varies accordingly to the number of players). The type of good shows in which category the player will rise: for each good of the type, the player climbs one spot in that good row. The player(s) in the top position in each type of good receive 10 money, the second, gets 5. Also, if the player reaches one of the top three spots of each row, he will get bonus: 5, 10 or 20 money - higher = bigger bonus. You can see this is a Knizia's game!

The objetic, of course, is to be the merchant with the most money in the end. Since the game doesn't use tokens for money, the sense of "no, I'm losing points in each bid!" is present and is part of the fun.

The player in turn can take, from the bag, one, two or three goods - one at a time. He can start the auction with one or two, but if he take out the third good, an auction [u]must[/u] happen. The players bid in clockwise order. The winner takes all the goods auctioned and put in his ship. The ship has space for 5 goods - the player cannot bid in a lot that has more goods than the spaces still empty in the ship. The rounds ends when there is only one player with room in the ship for goods - if there is still goods in the bag, this last player can take out up to three goods (depending on how much empty spaces there are in the ship) and can take them for free.

After the end of the round, the players compare the goods in the ships to see the most valuable ones. Then the players climb in the row of the goods they purchase and take the points if in first or second in the row.

Repeat this for two more rounds and the game is done!

All in all, a great auction game - at first we didn't know the value of things, but by the second day (round) the bids became more fierce and the type of good and the position of the players in the rows, start to matter a lot. That 20 bonus money for reaching the topmost position in a row of one of the goods is huge! The game normally lasts around the announced 45 minutes, as the clever single bid for each player takes away the long auctions, with players  over-analysing an increasing the bid by $1 several times.

Is [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/46/medici]Medici[/url] better than [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/12/ra]Ra[/url] or [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/118/modern-art]Modern Art[/url]? Yes and no. No for [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/12/ra]Ra[/url], as the auction in [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/12/ra]Ra[/url] is a thing of beauty. And yes for [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/118/modern-art]Modern Art[/url], because in spite of the many types of auctions in [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/118/modern-art]Modern Art[/url], which make the plays always feel fresh, [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/46/medici]Medici[/url] is better, being faster, tenser and more balanced, as the money go to no other player, meaning an overbid isn't twice bad.

Yet, while I [b]love[/b] [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/12/ra]Ra[/url], I just [b]like[/b] [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/46/medici]Medici[/url], but it is a good deal of like - and with each play, it grows in my preferences.

RATING: 4
Medici ist relativ kurzweilig, man kann es locker innerhalb von einer Stunde spielen. Am Anfang kann man ziemlich leicht einschätzen, was die Versteigerung gerade für einen selbst wert ist, in späteren runden kann man es dann sehr leicht berechnen, gegen Ende des Spiels gehört schon etwas mehr Rechnerei hinzu. Gerade die Tatsache, dass man mit Siegpunkten bezahlt, führt dazu, dass man schon sehr genau rechnen sollte. Aus dem Bauch heraus - Spieler werden sicherlich ihren Spass an dem Spiel, aber wenig siegchancen haben. Ein paar Mitspieler haben sich sehr an Modern Art erinnert gefühlt, dieses Spiel habe ich leider noch nicht gespielt. Schade finde ich, dass es keine wirkliche Versteigerung ist, sondern jeder nur ein Gebot hat. Aber gerade dies bringt dennoch einen gewissen Reiz hinein, da man abschätzen muss, was eine Auktion einem selbst wert ist, was sie anderen Wert sein könnte, und wenn man nur versucht den Preis in die Höhe zu treiben, kann man sich sehr schnell selbst in Bredouille bringen. Mir hat es gefallen, für einen netten geselligen Spieleabend mit Wenigspielern würde ich es jedoch eher nicht empfehlen, sondern eher Leuten, die Knizia Spiele kennen und mögen. Der ganz besondere Reiz fehlt mir bei Medici. Es ist einfach ein Versteigerungsspiel. Aber ein gutes.
