RATING: 8
The textbook definition of elegance- as usual Reiner makes an interesting, tense and fun game out of very simple rules. Comparing this to RA, they both have a push-your-luck element where you have to decide how much you're willing to pay for what's available now, in light of the fact that something (better? worse?) might come up later. In Medici you can bid any amount, as opposed to RA where your sun tiles determine the bids available to you. I think this constraint in RA makes it more accessible, but I personally like the open-ended bidding of Medici more. That being said, RA is much better with 3 players and Medici rules with 5. Only at 4 is there really competition between these games. I recommend owning both, personally. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I HATE THIS GAME.  No matter how often I play it, I can never figure out how to valuate a given set of cards.  It's worth x to me, y to you, z to that person over there, q to her, d to him ... GRARGH.  Every time I think I've made the perfect bid on something, I either hear cries of "THAT much??", or the next person jumps on it for a seeming bargain.

I LOVE THIS GAME.  I think it's cool how the ruleset is just a ruleset, and that the game itself lies in the decisions that players make during the course of the game.  The once-around bid is awesome, and forces players to decide immediately how valuable a particular set of cards is to everyone, and based on what other players have chosen, the value maybe vastly different to everyone.  It's very Sackson-esque, both in its simplicity of rules and complexity of scoring.

4 plays: 5 players
3 plays: 6 players

RATING: 7
I've heard this described as an archetypal auction game, and I can certainly see that. I didn't like the specialization, because it seems that by the end of the game there were lots that were only good for a player or maybe 2, and in a multiplayer game, there's only so much you're willing to spend to take one for the team to prevent someone from having something. It's a good game, but I can't see myself wanting to play it over things like Ra or For Sale.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Auction game mechanic is simple and easy to teach, but creates much tension. There is therefore very high player interaction with little downtime. Even if a player is already out of a day's remaining auctions (hold completely filled), that player will remain interested in observing the rest of the auctions. There is a small but not insignificant amount of luck for the player who starts filling up his/her hold last, which can be rather irritating.

The game does not work well with smaller player counts. With fewer players, one does not get as much opportunity to wait for a particular type of commodity to turn up. With a number of commodity cards removed, the game is also more random. The importance of turn order and how it affects the auctions is also downplayed with fewer players. Scalability is therefore on the low side and it really needs a greater number of players to be fun.

Game board design of the older editions leaves much to be desired, though the game components are quite nice. The latest edition resolves most of these issues.

RATING: 6
There's a good amount of luck in the draws in this one, but it plays fast and has some nice tactical play.

My one complaint is that I have the Rio Grande version that has the *worst* graphic redesign that I've ever witnessed.  There is no color coordination to tie the goods pyramids to the related goods cards.  You have to identify the pyramid by the pictures, which is not as easy as it sounds.  Also the writing on the cards is surprisingly hard to read. Finally, they skip every other number along the scoring track in a way that makes it very awkward to keep score.

By comparison, the original Amigo version has a clearer scoring track and easy to match-up goods pyramids.  Sigh...

RATING: 7.5
Let me preface this by saying that I'm not a big fan of auctions, so for Medici to not completely turn me off definitely speaks volume of its quality.  The once around bidding is brilliant by reducing the downtime and keeping the game from dragging.  It is true that the ability to count cards to get an idea of what's left in the deck does take some of the tension away, but not excessively so to rob you of the difficult choice between setting someone up to take him out of the auction or grabbing the set for yourself.

A very good experience for six players and I definitely would not mind exploring this further.  I look forward to revisiting Medici again.
